From ronabop at gmail.com  Mon Aug 25 11:57:55 2008
From: ronabop at gmail.com (Ron Chmara)
Date: Mon, 25 Aug 2008 11:57:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <20080825151132.GB2455@sentinelchicken.org>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0808250748370.2704@orange.madboa.com>
	<20080825151132.GB2455@sentinelchicken.org>
Message-ID: <48B300B3.7000707@gmail.com>

Tim wrote:
> I make a living out of breaking into
> websites and something about the PHP platform encourages insecure coding
> practicies.
That "something" would be the same "something" that Gimp and Photoshop 
have in encouraging bad design, that Word has in encouraging bad 
writing, and that Excel has in bad accounting practices....

For that matter, it's the very same "something" that a hammer has in 
common with bad building practices... people who aren't skilled in using 
a tool for a trade can, and will, often use it wrong (or poorly).

A tool which is readily available, accessible, and simple to use, tends 
to lead to mis-use in the wrong hands.

I suppose one can blame the tool *itself* for being used wrong, but it 
seems to me like that might be placing the blame poorly.

Of course, there are coding tools available which have lots of safety 
checks built in, but such tools are kinda like... hmmm... like hammers 
that don't allow you to swing them too fast, that require you to declare 
*exactly* where you are going to swing it, and at what target, and how 
hard, before you can even pick it up, and will fail to work at all 
unless you are wearing safety goggles and work gloves ahead of time.

For a seasoned building professional, such a hammer would be a serious 
annoyance to use. For somebody who is new to pounding nails, such a 
hammer might prevent them from injuring themselves, until they have 
enough experience to remove the "training wheels".

Sadly, what seems to have happened with PHP is that many people assumed 
that because they could swing a hammer, they then knew enough to build 
their own houses.

-Bop




From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Aug 25 12:13:32 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

# from Rich Shepard
# on Monday 25 August 2008 10:29:

>On Mon, 25 Aug 2008, Bill Thoen wrote:
>> I don't want ?to make a boring little html read-only web page. I'd
>> like to use my server to its fullest potential and not be
>> intimidated by the predators out there.
>
>? ?Allow me to suggest that you look at established frameworks for web
> sites that are based on scripting languages other than PHP. We're
> using Ruby on Rails for one service, and I know there are a bunch of
> Python-based frameworks with a lot of support and experience.

The question for Bill is whether he's looking for a web framework or a 
templating language.  The Catalyst framework is very mature, and built 
on top of secure code.

  http://www.catalystframework.org/

You can make a security hole with any programming language that has I/O, 
but Perl gives you a lot more tools for sanitizing user input, as well 
as syntax which allows you to treat a parameter as atomic rather than 
needing to meta-escape it (e.g. open, system, etc.)

Typically, if you're trying to change from php to any sort of framework, 
you'll find that things are done rather differently.  These tools tend 
to start with a config file (not just some code in a file which happens 
to be named "config"), a database, schema, and some templates.  
The "but I just want to ..." entry point isn't as easy to find as it is 
with php, but most of the developers have learned that any non-trivial 
application grows to the point where it needs these things -- which 
means that if you didn't start with them you end up with a buggy, 
ad-hoc reimplementation of them anyway.

If a framework is a lot to dive into and you're not trying to "engineer 
an application", maybe CGI::Simple is a good place to start.  
HTML::Mason gives you the template+language notion that php has, but 
that creates the same sort of mess that php does if you just set off on 
the path of creating a template with some embedded code.

--Eric

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Mon Aug 25 12:43:14 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Mon, 25 Aug 2008 12:43:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <48B300B3.7000707@gmail.com>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>
	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0808250748370.2704@orange.madboa.com>
	<20080825151132.GB2455@sentinelchicken.org>
	<48B300B3.7000707@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20080825194314.GE2455@sentinelchicken.org>

> I suppose one can blame the tool *itself* for being used wrong, but it 
> seems to me like that might be placing the blame poorly.
> 
> Of course, there are coding tools available which have lots of safety 
> checks built in [...]

I agree with your general premise that nerfing a tool to keep the kids
from hurting themselves also hinders the adults from getting things
done.  However, I'm not talking about using nerfed tools.

What I'm talking about is default behavior, and in the physical world,
there aren't many good analogies.  

Let me give an example: mandating prepared statements to send SQL
queries keeps users from *typically* shooting themselves in the foot.
This is because all dynamic arguments are automatically treated as data,
not SQL syntax.  Can users still shoot themselves in the foot if they
want to, and create SQL injection vulnerabilities?  Sure!  Just
dynamically construct the query and send it to the prepared statement
interface with no dynamic parameters.  This tends to be more work
though, so the typical newbie probably won't bother doing this.  The
most straight-forward way of getting the job done is the secure way.

The same goes for dynamic page templates.  Provide the user a templating
language which automatically HTML encodes dynamic parameters when
they're included in the page by *default*.  However, there should be a
way to override that behavior and explicitly say: "Hey, I want HTML to
be included here.  I've encoded things myself, trust me!"

Notice I didn't mention anything about input validation.  Far too many
programmers and even security professionals seem to think that input
validation is a panacea.  It isn't.  It is a good mitigation for a
number of vulnerabilities, but it also gets in the way of proper
application behavior in many cases and places business requirements at
odds with security.  Data encoding is almost always the correct answer
to preventing injection vulnerabilities and doesn't get in the way of
business requirements.

Hope that helps explain my position,
tim

you're going to end up in either LAMP, Java or Microsoft.

Security has nothing to do with the language or the framework. It's
something you have to design in from the ground floor and test
rigorously and constantly.

Hello Ed,

> Security has nothing to do with the language or the framework. 

I beg to differ.

How often do you see a Java application that's vulnerable to SQL
injection via a numeric field?  How often do you see a similar PHP
application be vulnerable to injection via a numeric field?  I'm sure
those numbers would differ, for the sole reason that Java is strictly
typed, and PHP isn't.  By nature, programmers will most often cast that
input parameter to an Integer in Java, just because that's the most
logical thing to do.

Strictly typed languages have their drawbacks, but this is just meant to
illustrate that language does matter when it comes to the security of
*typical, real-world* applications.

How often do you see Python applications vulnerable to remote file
include vulnerabilities?  How often with PHP?  Yeah... PHP basically
invented that class of vulnerability.  Complete and utter foolishness on
the PHP developers' part, but now we're stuck with it.

When it comes to real-world applications, the platform does matter
because most programmers suck.


> It's
> something you have to design in from the ground floor and test
> rigorously and constantly.

Yes.

Regardless of the platform and language, you can always shoot yourself
in the foot.  I'm frequently amazed by how much work some programmers
will put into making their apps insecure.  Design security into your
apps, and test, test, test.

cheers,
tim


From ronabop at gmail.com  Mon Aug 25 14:59:26 2008
From: ronabop at gmail.com (Ron Chmara)
Date: Mon, 25 Aug 2008 14:59:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <20080825194314.GE2455@sentinelchicken.org>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0808250748370.2704@orange.madboa.com>	<20080825151132.GB2455@sentinelchicken.org>	<48B300B3.7000707@gmail.com>
	<20080825194314.GE2455@sentinelchicken.org>
Message-ID: <48B32B3E.8060306@gmail.com>

Tim wrote:
>> I suppose one can blame the tool *itself* for being used wrong, but it 
>> seems to me like that might be placing the blame poorly.
>>
>> Of course, there are coding tools available which have lots of safety 
>> checks built in [...]
>>     
>
> I agree with your general premise that nerfing a tool to keep the kids
> from hurting themselves also hinders the adults from getting things
> done.  However, I'm not talking about using nerfed tools....
> ...mandating prepared statements to send SQL
> queries keeps users from *typically* shooting themselves in the foot...
>   

One person's "mandat[ed] prepared statement" is another person's 
nerfing.... same thing goes for "nerfing" a language in such a way that 
it requires casting to specific data types (or requires variables to be 
initialized at all), or defaulting to a chroot (or similar) kind of 
jail, or enforces any kind of separation between display and logic (etc. 
etc.).

There are perfectly sane, and reasonable, arguments for doing such 
things, but philosophically, PHP went more in the direction of "making 
web programming easier for C developers", rather than "making dynamic 
web page content easier for HTML developers". Put another way, PHP 
generally *expected* a certain level of expertise, experience, and 
diligence on the part of software authors.

As an argument/example of the philosophical question, and approach the 
language took: "Should a server scripting language be able to simply 
read local files, or include files remotely from another server, without 
having to jump through a lot of hoops"? The default for PHP for a long 
time was to make such things easy for a programmer.

Of course, the counter argument to making this easy is noted in this 
very thread, as PHP was allowed to easily read /etc/passwd (it's a file 
on the hard disk, why shouldn't PHP be able to read it?...), and yet, 
there was an implied expectation that PHP "shouldn't be able to do 
that". Therin lies the rub, whether or not a feature like that is a 
desirable default behavior. ;)

-Bop

M. Edward (Ed) Borasky (znmeb at cesmail.net) typed this ...
> On Mon, 2008-08-25 at 10:29 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> >    Allow me to suggest that you look at established frameworks for web sites
> > that are based on scripting languages other than PHP. We're using Ruby on
> > Rails for one service, and I know there are a bunch of Python-based
> > frameworks with a lot of support and experience.
> 
> I'm not terribly impressed with the "security features" of Ruby on
> Rails. For the most part, they consist of warnings in the major books on
> some simple things not to do. 

Security in Rails apps is the responsibility of the programmer, who is
responsible for defining constraints. Rails provides easy-to-use tools
for implementing those constraints in a well-tested manner. 

Using multiple databases forces the Rails programmer to define queries
in a way that causes the framework to use a 'prepare' under the hood. If
you use only one some of the pressure is off and you can screw it up if
you want...

> Moreover, I don't consider Ruby on Rails
> "established" in any sense of the word. If you want "established",
> you're going to end up in either LAMP, Java or Microsoft.

Absolutely true. 4 years is not long enough to be considered mature.
However, Rails has a smart and prolific following, and bugs are fixed
promptly. There have been no security patches to the latest stable Rails
release in the 3-4 months since it exited beta. That's not very long,
but consider that every previous release had a security release within a
week or two. 

> Security has nothing to do with the language or the framework. It's
> something you have to design in from the ground floor and test
> rigorously and constantly.

I disagree, and agree with previously presented arguments: the framework
can support good practices and make bad practices more difficult. That's
one of the things a framework should do for you: support good practices.

PHP (which I also use and like, in the right context) supports nothing
and impedes nothing; it is completely neutral. CakePHP (a Rails clone)
supports good practices and makes bad practices difficult, up to a
point. Many frameworks have accepted that mantle.

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Aug 25 15:56:59 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Mon, 25 Aug 2008 15:56:59 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <48B32B3E.8060306@gmail.com>

# from Ron Chmara
# on Monday 25 August 2008 14:59:

>Of course, the counter argument to making this easy is noted in this
>very thread, as PHP was allowed to easily read /etc/passwd (it's a
> file on the hard disk, why shouldn't PHP be able to read it?...), and
> yet, there was an implied expectation that PHP "shouldn't be able to
> do that".

That's not really it.  If you do open(my $fh, '<', '/etc/passwd'), you 
get that.  If you're just trying to display some bit of text that's 
supposed to be next to you in the webroot, your tool (especially a very 
web-centric tool) should give you a default way to do that which limits 
the filenames to within the webroot.  That's not "nerf", it's more a 
matter of providing expressions which fit the context.  Given a 
function for "render this content as a part of my output", allowing
"this" to point at ../../etc/ is clearly (in this context) the "wrong 
thing" because our context says these things are supposed to be within 
a narrow set of locations which don't include /etc/.

In most languages, you could probably use a lower-level function to 
fetch data from /etc/passwd, but at that point, you're doing something 
different.  The framework should give you convenient tools for doing 
the sensible thing.  It can still give you plenty of rope, but handing 
me a neatly coiled rope is completely different from handing me a 
bucket full of knots.

--Eric

Tim wrote:
> Hello Ed,
>
>   
>> Security has nothing to do with the language or the framework. 
>>     
>
> I beg to differ.
>
> How often do you see a Java application that's vulnerable to SQL
> injection via a numeric field?

Not to mix apples and oranges, but I don't see a lot of numeric fields 
on the web... it's almost all text/html. ;)

(Side note for those who don't get the code joke: html GET and POST is 
all text, so the typing/casting debate for web apps is really about how 
a programming language should *re-cast* input/output data, and/or handle 
internal variables.... okay, so maybe it's not that funny, as folks who 
*don't* know about this simple distinction are often the ones who are 
surprised when their "numeric" or "boolean" input field is used for an 
SQL injection string.)

>  How often do you see a similar PHP
> application be vulnerable to injection via a numeric field?  I'm sure
> those numbers would differ, for the sole reason that Java is strictly
> typed, and PHP isn't.  By nature, programmers will most often cast that
> input parameter to an Integer in Java, just because that's the most
> logical thing to do.
>   

A programmer used to the benefits of casting will do so, regardless of 
PHP or Java or perl or <whatever>.... a programmer who *doesn't* clean 
up their inputs can make that mistake regardless of the language. Where 
strongly/strictly-typed languages do add value/benefit is enforcing the 
most *minimal* discipline.....(Oblig xkcd: http://xkcd.com/327/ ). Of 
course, casting is a mere drop in the bucket of things to look at ('DROP 
TABLES' is a totally valid string... ;-) ), as there are boundary 
issues, race issues, privilege issues, yada yada....


> Strictly typed languages have their drawbacks, but this is just meant to
> illustrate that language does matter when it comes to the security of
> *typical, real-world* applications.
>   

The cost of, and choice of, languages can *influence* security, but I 
think this may be an argument that's more about the developers (and the 
languages they choose), rather than the languages (and the developers 
who choose them)... AKA "it's possible to write FORTRAN in any 
language". If a developer is numb/sloppy with respect to security 
issues, no language can magically fix that problem for them, and in the 
"real-world", there are a lot of different kinds of developers....


> How often do you see Python applications vulnerable to remote file
> include vulnerabilities?

Python can't easily read local or remote files depending on dynamic 
input arguments? That's gotta hurt. ;)
(I kid... I hope. Can python really not do this?)

>  How often with PHP?  Yeah... PHP basically
> invented that class of vulnerability.  Complete and utter foolishness on
> the PHP developers' part, but now we're stuck with it.
>   

Where that "feature" really went south was people writing dynamic 
loaders without any forethought of what their input arguments needed to 
be restricted to. Those "dynamic loaders" got stuck in a lot of 
high-profile projects, and the rest is history.

> When it comes to real-world applications, the platform does matter
> because most programmers suck.
>   
I've seen less-than-ideal PHP/Java coders write the exact same problems 
into both languages. I've also seen a heck of a lot more bad public PHP 
code than Java code (putting aside the major DoS/memory issues that many 
Java apps seem to love). What I think makes a *huge* difference is the 
number of long term, professional, coders in various arenas, as compared 
to hobbyists and enthusiasts. PHP is where a lot of people seem to cut 
their web-app security teeth the hard way, where folks into Python, 
Ruby, Perl (etc.) tend to have a few more years of "making mistakes" 
under their belt.

-Bop

	<20080825201300.GF2455@sentinelchicken.org>

Message-ID: <20080825235053.GG2455@sentinelchicken.org>


> [...]

Most of your responses up to this point in your last message seem to be
of the form "Well, it is still *possible*, regardless of
language/platform to write bad code".  Yes, I don't dispute this.  I've
seen Java developers fail to cast integer arguments and have them
inject.  The point is, if you actually sit down and test applications
like this on various platforms for a living, you'll find that some
platforms just have many fewer of these stupid mistakes.  I'm not
talking about theory, I'm talking about practice.


> Where that "feature" really went south was people writing dynamic 
> loaders without any forethought of what their input arguments needed to 
> be restricted to. Those "dynamic loaders" got stuck in a lot of 
> high-profile projects, and the rest is history.

Including remote files as code is a dumb idea, regardless of language.
If you really had to do this in a distributed application environment,
you're better off using a network filesystem.

Of course Python can pull down a remote file and eval it in one of
several ways.  Making that kind of functionality a part of "import ..."
would be sheer idiocy.  Period.  Consider the principle of least
surprise (http://www.faqs.org/docs/artu/ch11s01.html).


> > When it comes to real-world applications, the platform does matter
> > because most programmers suck.
> > 
> I've seen less-than-ideal PHP/Java coders write the exact same problems 
> into both languages. I've also seen a heck of a lot more bad public PHP 
> code than Java code (putting aside the major DoS/memory issues that many 
> Java apps seem to love). What I think makes a *huge* difference is the 
> number of long term, professional, coders in various arenas, as compared 
> to hobbyists and enthusiasts. PHP is where a lot of people seem to cut 
> their web-app security teeth the hard way, where folks into Python, 
> Ruby, Perl (etc.) tend to have a few more years of "making mistakes" 
> under their belt.

True, maybe there are just more bad programmers working on PHP apps.
Is that because it's so easy to learn, or because experienced
programmers realize it's an unsafe platform?


tim


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Aug 25 17:28:56 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 25 Aug 2008 17:28:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Has anyone here used DKIM or Domain Key with their mail
	server?
Message-ID: <20080826002856.GA7219@gate.kl-ic.com>


Domain Key from Yah-o-o is being absorbed into the IETF's DKIM standard. 
There is apparently a Perl module that can be added to postfix to
implement this on outbound email.  This is apparently a scheme to
sign emails with the OpenSSL private key so that a recipient MTA
can check it with the site's public key. 

There is a Perl module ( http://dkimproxy.sourceforge.net/ ) that
supposedly implements this.

Questions: (1) Is this worth the effort?  (2) Is it a security risk?
(3) is the dkimproxy module sound?  (4) can you use a self-signed
cert for this, or do you need an official ($$$) cert from places
like Thawte or Verisign?  

Keith

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Aug 25 20:11:05 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

# from Keith Lofstrom
# on Monday 25 August 2008 17:28:

>Questions: (1) Is this worth the effort? ?(2) Is it a security risk?
>(3) is the dkimproxy module sound? ?(4) can you use a self-signed
>cert for this

These are good questions.

(3) At a glance, the Perl looks well written and appears to use solid 
CPAN modules to handle the crypto bits.  Not sure what possessed them 
to use autoconf or where exactly the supposed prefork is happening.

(1) As far as the DKIM protocol itself, it seems to be optimized toward 
selling processors, but maybe the hashing and signature checking is 
going to be faster than the amount work it currently takes to run 
spamassassin?

(4) As for buying yourself some "trust", the RFC might yield to a more 
careful reading, but seems to say the receiver MAY choose which certs 
to trust (and how), while at the same time saying that authority can be 
inferred via the DNS.

From linux at chrisroberts.org  Tue Aug 26 11:14:32 2008

After some webmails started rejecting emails from me I set up citadel to 
use the perl dkimproxy. Took a few minutes to set up and it has been 
running solid for awhile now. Granted, there's not a lot of mail going 
out, but it's been working just fine. You don't need an official cert. 
When the mail is received and they are checking the domain keys, they 
just see if your email matches the key in your DNS entry.

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Tue Aug 26 11:48:52 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Tue, 26 Aug 2008 11:48:52 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <BA8EF3F9-11AA-450F-9EC0-9382ACCA29DA@gmail.com>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>
	<200808251556.59351.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<BA8EF3F9-11AA-450F-9EC0-9382ACCA29DA@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200808261148.52601.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Ronald Chmara
# on Tuesday 26 August 2008 03:04:

>>If you're just trying to display some bit of text that's
>> supposed to be next to you in the webroot, your tool (especially a ?
>> very web-centric tool) should give you a default way to do that which ?
>> limits the filenames to within the webroot.
>
>If your webroot for the application is /etc/, because your particular
> ? application is a sysadmin application, /etc/passwd being read-
> accessible makes perfect sense.

Yes.  I would hope you were doing some authentication in that case.  But 
still, what I said is that you want a method for addressing filenames 
within the webroot which throws an error when handed an out-of-bounds 
filename.  If you actually set the webroot to /etc/ (though I wouldn't 
do this, even if it were a sysadmin app), well that isn't 
out-of-bounds, is it?

>> ?The framework should give you convenient tools for doing
>> the sensible thing. ?It can still give you plenty of rope, but
>> handing me a neatly coiled rope is completely different from handing
>> me a bucket full of knots.
>
>You somewhat lost me, but I would guess that the general debate ?
>focuses on the issue of whether PHP/perl/python/C/java/bash/
><whatever> should be limited to being a jailed "DocumentRoot" ?
>language, or a language that can inherit the permissions of a parent ?
>process.

No.  That's the wrong debate.  One *could* have a framework (like the 
google appengine will supposedly be) which eliminates I/O methods from 
the language or replaces them with virtualized and managed code.  If 
you want to provide shared hosting to untrusted programmers, this is 
what you need to do.  Or you could use javascript on the server.  You 
still have a chance of leaking information.

But in general, the language itself doesn't need to jail you.  Rather, 
it (or a module/framework) should provide a good set of functions for 
doing the right thing.  In this case, the context is "web application", 
so your DTRT methods need to take the webroot and etc into account.  If 
the tool doesn't make it absolutely easy to do the right thing, it 
probably isn't the right tool.

In the PHP case, you have a lot of core language functions which are 
specifically designed for use in web applications, yet they all tend to 
place convenience over security (so much so that you would think this 
was some kind of templating language hacked together in an afternoon's 
worth of Perl.)  Having both would not be so difficult, but the 
language design doesn't go there.  It would have been simple to make 
the potentially insecure functionality require a bit of extra 
code/input (hey, just have yet another function with the 
prefix 'unsafe_' -- what's 400 more functions in the core syntax?), but 
what you got is the opposite:  extra coding will get you potentially 
secure functionality.

So, maybe the developers which PHP attracts would actually be better off 
with a javascript or otherwise jailed+stripped+managed framework?

--Eric

clap.

From ronabop at gmail.com  Tue Aug 26 14:11:29 2008
From: ronabop at gmail.com (Ron Chmara)
Date: Tue, 26 Aug 2008 14:11:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <20080825235053.GG2455@sentinelchicken.org>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0808250748370.2704@orange.madboa.com>	<20080825151132.GB2455@sentinelchicken.org>	<48B2E5A9.6050304@gisnet.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0808251025490.3029@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<1219694110.15822.44.camel@DreamScape>	<20080825201300.GF2455@sentinelchicken.org>	<48B33DD4.6060905@gmail.com>
	<20080825235053.GG2455@sentinelchicken.org>

Tim wrote:
>> [...]
>> Most of your responses up to this point in your last message seem to be
>> of the form "Well, it is still *possible*, regardless of
>> language/platform to write bad code".  Yes, I don't dispute this.  I've
>> seen Java developers fail to cast integer arguments and have them
>> inject.  The point is, if you actually sit down and test applications
>> like this on various platforms for a living, you'll find that some
>> platforms just have many fewer of these stupid mistakes.  I'm not
>> talking about theory, I'm talking about practice.
>>     
Each platform seems to have its frequent failings, and benefits, in real 
world usage, yes.... ;)

Choices about given platforms will often reflect which benefits are 
desired, and which failings are considered marginally acceptable. To 
pick on my own stereotypes about the various stacks for a moment, Java 
stacks are great if you have absolutely unlimited budget and hardware 
resources, perl is fantastic if you never, ever, have to maintain or 
improve code of even slightly moderate complexity, Ruby/Rails (and 
similar conceptual "framework" stacks) are super-cool if you never need 
to do anything outside of the existing framework, and PHP is just spiffy 
if you don't mind regularly re-writing or upgrading other people's (or 
your own) first/second/third draft sloppy code...

Yes, these are my own grossly exaggerated generalizations, ones I 
learned the hard way from my own real world situations. There are 
exceptions. :) As with many stereotypes and prejudices, not being able 
to see past one's own biases and preconceptions can also be a real 
problem. ;)

> Of course Python can pull down a remote file and eval it in one of
> several ways.  Making that kind of functionality a part of "import ..."
> would be sheer idiocy.  Period.  Consider the principle of least
> surprise (http://www.faqs.org/docs/artu/ch11s01.html).
>   
As an argument, an include/import command that *doesn't*, oh, eval the 
code that was just imported seems totally silly, as I really don't see 
what would make sense about a C, PHP, (whatever) program having to 
include hundreds of header files, and than *also* having to eval those 
files in another, separate, step. I'm already including the code for 
pete's sake, why the heck should I have to first include *and* then eval it?

#include stdio.c
#eval stdio.c
?

It just seems absurd and nonsensical to me. If all somebody wanted to do 
in PHP was dump a file into output, fpassthru() does the trick, but 
forcing all includes/imports (or even just ones with specific contexts?) 
to run a separate, additional, step, just to acknowledge that the 
requested imported code/file is, indeed, *requested imported 
code/files*.... would seem to be a rather astonishing behavior.

Consider, for a second, the following meta-code, with the "convenience" 
of eval() added for giggles:

function SuperKewlLoader ($user_submitted_data_argument){
    $temp_blob = import ($user_submitted_data_argument);
    eval ($temp_blob);
}

I would argue that the underlying code problem under discussion *isn't* 
that the PHP import command is doing the wrong thing by, well, importing 
and evaluating code.

The way I see it, it really doesn't matter if the file is coming from a 
"bad user's" file on a local physical hard disk, a bad user file hosted 
via NFS or otherwise on on a SAN, or a bad user file fetched from 
halfway around the world via http/ftp/vpn 
tunnel/snail-mail/pigeons/whatever, the problem is that dirty 
user-submitted data is *intentionally* being allowed to control what 
code is being imported and executed. The argument could be a local disk 
file://home/eviluser/badness.php, a remote 
http://www.example.com/baduser.php, an uploaded /tmp/badness.php, a 
courier pigeon://african/swallow/badness.php, whatever.

As soon as a given software author decided that remote end-users could 
be allowed to submit their own arguments about what files/code were 
being imported and executed, the game was over. It doesn't matter if the 
underlying language is PHP, C, Java, Python, it's all the same result. 
Some languages and platforms may make it *harder* to do such a 
boneheaded thing ("I see you are trying to let the world access and 
execute any file on your disk, Cancel or Allow?"), but the fault there 
isn't really the language, it's with the user who clicks "Allow", or in 
this discussion, the programmer who *added* that (rather questionable) 
publicly available feature to their software.

>>  What I think makes a *huge* difference is the 
>> number of long term, professional, coders in various arenas, as compared 
>> to hobbyists and enthusiasts. PHP is where a lot of people seem to cut 
>> their web-app security teeth the hard way, where folks into Python, 
>> Ruby, Perl (etc.) tend to have a few more years of "making mistakes" 
>> under their belt.    
>
> True, maybe there are just more bad programmers working on PHP apps.
> Is that because it's so easy to learn, or because experienced
> programmers realize it's an unsafe platform?

I think PHP is easy enough to learn that people who have little to no 
long-term experience in making "the usual mistakes" can quickly pick it 
up, and once they get trained or otherwise used to avoiding typical 
mistakes, they will will seek out workflows and methods that enable 
avoiding those same mistakes.

In some cases, they'll avoid using PHP at all, and rely on other 
environments and workflows that attempt to remove those common mistakes. 
In other cases, they'll learn the kinds of PHP coding disciplines and 
techniques that avoid the mistakes... but in other cases, well, they'll 
just keep on making mistakes, and semi-patching with bad ideas and more 
mistakes, until their code is a nightmare of hacks, kludges, and endless 
exploit postings on bugtraq.

The last case seems to be the source of PHP's sometimes unfavorable 
reputation. :)

-Bop

> As an argument, an include/import command that *doesn't*, oh, eval the 
> code that was just imported seems totally silly, as I really don't see 
> what would make sense about a C, PHP, (whatever) program having to 
> include hundreds of header files, and than *also* having to eval those 
> files in another, separate, step. I'm already including the code for 
> pete's sake, why the heck should I have to first include *and* then eval it?
> 
> #include stdio.c
> #eval stdio.c
> ?
> 
> It just seems absurd and nonsensical to me. If all somebody wanted to do 
> in PHP was dump a file into output, fpassthru() does the trick, but 
> forcing all includes/imports (or even just ones with specific contexts?) 
> to run a separate, additional, step, just to acknowledge that the 
> requested imported code/file is, indeed, *requested imported 
> code/files*.... would seem to be a rather astonishing behavior.
> 
> Consider, for a second, the following meta-code, with the "convenience" 
> of eval() added for giggles:
> 
> function SuperKewlLoader ($user_submitted_data_argument){
>     $temp_blob = import ($user_submitted_data_argument);
>     eval ($temp_blob);
> }
> 
> I would argue that the underlying code problem under discussion *isn't* 
> that the PHP import command is doing the wrong thing by, well, importing 
> and evaluating code.

My argument isn't that an imported file shouldn't be eval()ed.  My
argument is that a language macro should be able to include files from
remote systems over protocols like HTTP.  Even without the security
implications, this is a huge hack for a language to have to implement an
HTTP client just to include a file as code.  The fact that PHP has this
behavior is a big surprise to many programmers.  A rather nasty surprise
that results in immediate compromise if you want to have dynamically
included files at all.


> The way I see it, it really doesn't matter if the file is coming from a 
> "bad user's" file on a local physical hard disk, a bad user file hosted 
> via NFS or otherwise on on a SAN, or a bad user file fetched from 
> halfway around the world via http/ftp/vpn 
> tunnel/snail-mail/pigeons/whatever, the problem is that dirty 
> user-submitted data is *intentionally* being allowed to control what 
> code is being imported and executed. The argument could be a local disk 
> file://home/eviluser/badness.php, a remote 
> http://www.example.com/baduser.php, an uploaded /tmp/badness.php, a 
> courier pigeon://african/swallow/badness.php, whatever.
> 
> As soon as a given software author decided that remote end-users could 
> be allowed to submit their own arguments about what files/code were 
> being imported and executed, the game was over. It doesn't matter if the 
> underlying language is PHP, C, Java, Python, it's all the same result. 
> Some languages and platforms may make it *harder* to do such a 
> boneheaded thing ("I see you are trying to let the world access and 
> execute any file on your disk, Cancel or Allow?"), but the fault there 
> isn't really the language, it's with the user who clicks "Allow", or in 
> this discussion, the programmer who *added* that (rather questionable) 
> publicly available feature to their software.


Ok, so here's a separate issue.  I don't disagree with your assertion
that it's a bad idea to allow an untrusted user to specify which files
are included as code.  This is obviously a dangerous thing.  However,
software needs to *fail gracefully*.

Let me say this again: Secure software *fails gracefully*.  The fact
that PHP includes a remote file import macro causes many minor
vulnerabilities to develop into very severe ones.  The typical public
website doesn't allow one to upload files.  This makes it very difficult
to execute code even if you can specify the file to load.

Since most languages don't have this inane feature like PHP does,
programmers aren't used to having to be so careful about injections into
these macros.  This is a big surprise and violates the premise I
mentioned previously.

Sure, if you're a l33t programmer, and you care about writing secure
code, it doesn't matter since you'd just avoid this and other issues.
Unfortunately those are two huge assumptions, the original poster (not
to mention every other PHP app on sourceforge) have clearly
demonstrated.

tim


PS- I think I'm done with this thread.  I don't think I'll convince
    anyone of my views who haven't been convinced already.


From ronabop at gmail.com  Tue Aug 26 16:31:32 2008
From: ronabop at gmail.com (Ron Chmara)
Date: Tue, 26 Aug 2008 16:31:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <200808261148.52601.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>	<200808251556.59351.ewilhelm@cpan.org>	<BA8EF3F9-11AA-450F-9EC0-9382ACCA29DA@gmail.com>
	<200808261148.52601.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <48B49254.8010908@gmail.com>

Eric Wilhelm wrote:
> # from Ronald Chmara
> # on Tuesday 26 August 2008 03:04:
>   
>> If your webroot for the application is /etc/, because your particular
>>   application is a sysadmin application, /etc/passwd being read-
>> accessible makes perfect sense.
>>     
> Yes.  I would hope you were doing some authentication in that case.  But 
> still, what I said is that you want a method for addressing filenames 
> within the webroot which throws an error when handed an out-of-bounds 
> filename.  If you actually set the webroot to /etc/ (though I wouldn't 
> do this, even if it were a sysadmin app), well that isn't 
> out-of-bounds, is it?
>   
http://us.php.net/manual/en/features.safe-mode.php#ini.open-basedir

Unfortunately, what seems at first blush to be a fairly simple thing can 
get pretty complex, pretty fast (PHP's "safe_mode" has a whole, uhm, 
history.). Putting this level of restriction on a general default 
per-apache-process level, for example, can lead to hundreds of "allowed 
directories" in the web roots on a shared server available to PHP 
processes (one directory for each vhost), with possibly multiple PEAR 
include libraries for different users, so it's really something that 
should be handled on a per-script, per-program execution cycle, level 
(as required).... back to square one, though, if the programmers simply 
can't be bothered to write more secure code, or intentionally dodge the 
built-in features designed to keep themselves from shooting at their feet.

> In the PHP case, you have a lot of core language functions which are 
> specifically designed for use in web applications, yet they all tend to 
> place convenience over security (so much so that you would think this 
> was some kind of templating language hacked together in an afternoon's 
> worth of Perl.)
"all tend to..." might be a little broad....  :D as far as "convenience 
over security", that's up to the programmer. If, for example, a 
programmer wants to use prepared statements, transactions, and 
language-specific-db-string-escaping for SQL, they can. If they want 
straight raw SQL client, convenience, and speed, and are willing to 
suffer the potential drawbacks, they can.


> So, maybe the developers which PHP attracts would actually be better off 
> with a javascript or otherwise jailed+stripped+managed framework?
>   
Yeah, they'd never get bitten by XSS bugs that way, right?... ;)

I think the challenge is in trying to write a software application 
language that doesn't allow people to easily shoot themselves in the 
foot.... yet is still powerful enough to do all of the tasks they want, 
even if that task happens to be, uhm, easily shooting one's self in the 
foot.


-Bop


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Tue Aug 26 17:30:53 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Tue, 26 Aug 2008 17:30:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <48B49254.8010908@gmail.com>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>
	<200808261148.52601.ewilhelm@cpan.org> <48B49254.8010908@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200808261730.53637.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Ron Chmara
# on Tuesday 26 August 2008 16:31:

>>what I said is that you want a method for addressing filenames
>> within the webroot which throws an error when handed an
>> out-of-bounds filename. ?If you actually set the webroot to /etc/
>> (though I wouldn't do this, even if it were a sysadmin app), well
>> that isn't out-of-bounds, is it?
>> ?
>
>http://us.php.net/manual/en/features.safe-mode.php#ini.open-basedir
>
>Unfortunately, what seems at first blush to be a fairly simple thing
> can get pretty complex, pretty fast (PHP's "safe_mode" has a whole,
> uhm, history.). Putting this level of restriction on a general
> default per-apache-process level, for example, ...

Hey, I didn't say it was simple, but I also didn't say anything about 
having a configuration variable define the limits on fopen().  That 
sort of language feature just makes it hard to audit an application 
because now you have to also audit the configuration.  Things like 
this, register_globals, safe_mode, enable_exploits, allow_rootkit and 
such are a huge part of the reason that PHP is so horribly difficult to 
secure.

What I said was "a method for addressing filenames within the webroot". 
This has got nothing whatsoever to do with fopen() or the apache config 
outside of the fact that PHP fails to provide anything on that level of 
abstraction.

A much better way to do this is to have a 'webfile' object (subclassed 
from a 'file' object) which is guaranteed to throw an error if you try 
to do anything with one that contains the name of a file outside the 
webroot.

This would mean that, when reading some code, you could see "oh, he's 
using a 'file' object here.  That should be a 'webfile' object."

It would be quite simple to teach too -- "if you're doing something on 
the web, you always* want a webfile object".

(Aside:  If I suspected that exception-wary programmers would be reading 
it, "always" would say "almost always", but people who think like that 
would not have these troubles with security.)

Ah well, you would still get a bunch of useless tutorials telling you 
how to do it the wrong way anyway, but at least the language itself 
wouldn't be so gnarly.

--Eric

On Aug 26, 2008, at 2:41 PM, Tim wrote:
> Even without the security
> implications, this is a huge hack for a language to have to  
> implement an
> HTTP client just to include a file as code.  The fact that PHP has  
> this
> behavior is a big surprise to many programmers.  A rather nasty  
> surprise
> that results in immediate compromise if you want to have dynamically
> included files at all.

Oh dear. I hope you're sitting down.

Yes, PHP I/O wrappers allow data to be accessed via http, but it's  
not like an http client was randomly or haphazardly bolted onto PHP's  
include() function... include() uses PHP's stream wrappers.

PHP's stream wrappers allow including and accessing streams of data  
(like, oh, files) via (depending on the config/compile/libraries):
file, http, https, ftp, ftps, stdin, stdout, stderr, filter, memory,  
temp, zlib, bzip2, rfc2397, glob, ssh, ogg, expect, CURL (so, another  
round of http(s), but also DNS/POP/IMAP etc.)...

... and since 4.3.0, (released in 2002) programmers could also write  
their own wrappers to handle/manage any stream they added, so a php  
application could, say, include data from an IRC chatroom, load data  
from an IP-based POS cash register, etc etc. (Side note: PHP does  
sockets, too.)

My comment about getting data from pigeon://african/swallow/ 
badness.php was about a PHP I/O *feature*, not merely a jest.

It's kinda a big thing to leave ignored/unsecured, or allow  
potentially-malicious user input to have unguarded control over.

Now, as to *why* this little tidbit would, or could, surprise people,  
I'm guessing a lack of training/knowledge, or awareness of the  
feature set of PHP. Glossing over the I/O abilities of any  
programming language is kind of a bad thing.

>>
> Let me say this again: Secure software *fails gracefully*.  The fact
> that PHP includes a remote file import macro causes many minor
> vulnerabilities to develop into very severe ones.

Well, it's not *a* (singular) macro, it's part of PHP's core I/O to  
be able to get, and send, data on local and remote levels.

Arguments can, and have been, made, that PHP should (by default) be  
dumbed back down to I/O along the lines of, oh, bash scripts, but  
it's far too important a feature set to remove entirely.

> The typical public
> website doesn't allow one to upload files.  This makes it very  
> difficult
> to execute code even if you can specify the file to load.

As long as the website isn't allowing users to submit I/O requests,  
it's not a problem. ;)

Of course, if programmers don't realize that they're handing around  
and executing insecure I/O requests, oh yeah... big problem.

-Bop






From ronabop at gmail.com  Tue Aug 26 21:25:54 2008
From: ronabop at gmail.com (Ronald Chmara)
Date: Tue, 26 Aug 2008 21:25:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preventing Probes Like This
In-Reply-To: <200808261730.53637.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <48B29FE3.4020803@gisnet.com>
	<200808261148.52601.ewilhelm@cpan.org> <48B49254.8010908@gmail.com>

On Aug 26, 2008, at 5:30 PM, Eric Wilhelm wrote:
> A much better way to do this is to have a 'webfile' object (subclassed
> from a 'file' object) which is guaranteed to throw an error if you try
> to do anything with one that contains the name of a file outside the
> webroot.

Expected Issues:
Symlinks. Imports with changing mount points. Scripts that wget/exec/ 
mount (etc.) other code and readfile()/eval()/exec() it. PHP already  
using /usr/share/pear, /etc/php.ini, and a whole ton of code  
scattered around. Trying to keep/maintain a registry of thousands, or  
hundreds of thousands, of acceptable files/objects,  across thousands  
of servers, and their last "acceptable" load locations. Integrating  
with a meta-file abstraction, where you never know if the next "file"  
is coming from a disk, an IRC chat room, a user at a console, etc.

Many have seen this very monster, and then tried to slay the beast.  
Many have failed.

Patches Welcome. :)

(FWIW, (since it made me laugh) the "allow_rootkit", aka "safe_mode"  
stack, is being removed in PHP v6. Look at it as clearing the dead  
carcasses of "those who came before" being finally cleared from the  
battlefield.)

> Ah well, you would still get a bunch of useless tutorials telling you
> how to do it the wrong way anyway, but at least the language itself
> wouldn't be so gnarly.

Namespaces and custom import/loading of namespaces (which should be  
helpful to "pin" applications to using files/objects from allowed  
namespaces) is rolling out soon, along with some built-in garbage  
collection. (This will, of course, make the language slower overall,  
but hey, folks want it!)

There will still be a bunch of useless tutorials and horridly bad  
documentation and related notes, however. (Seriously, though, if  
anybody wants to start editing the PHP manual and notes, it's always  
a short-handed, thankless, gig, looking for volunteers... I did it  
for years, and learned an immense amount of what to do, and not to  
do, with PHP. )

:)

-Bop

# from Ronald Chmara
# on Tuesday 26 August 2008 21:25:

>>which is guaranteed to throw an error if you try
>> to do anything with one that contains the name of a file outside the
>> webroot.
>
>Expected Issues:
>Symlinks.

How are symlinks a problem other than that they could exist in the 
webroot?  The webroot is (of course) not writable by the httpd process, 
so this is unsurprisingly controllable and auditable.  Note that my 
suggested interface (e.g. a web-specific subclass of File::Fu) does not 
purport to be a cure-all, simply an easy way to not do most of "the 
absolutely wrong things" by default.

>Imports with changing mount points. 

Umm... what?

>Scripts that wget/exec/ 
>mount (etc.) other code and readfile()/eval()/exec() it.

Yeah, perl doesn't try to stop you from saying eval(`curl $user_input`), 
yet I don't see that one too often.

>PHP already using /usr/share/pear, /etc/php.ini ...
> 
>Many have seen this very monster, and then tried to slay the beast. ?
>Many have failed.

I think it's more like pandora's box than a monster.  If you have only 
one super-dwimmy interface to a critical feature (open, include, etc) 
which itself contains 100 misfeatures, the only way to excise the 
misfeatures is to break existing code.

But, given that it isn't installed, I have the fortunate position of not 
feeling any need to fix PHP.  I only need to convince new developers to 
use Perl.

A programming language / framework / module with the philosophy that "if 
you want something special, you have to ask for it" is much less likely 
to surprise unsuspecting users with amazing security holes, er 
features.

--Eric

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Aug 26 23:29:30 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 26 Aug 2008 23:29:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Verizon FIOS, swapping out the Actiontec cable modem
Message-ID: <20080827062930.GA15653@gate.kl-ic.com>


No questions here, just some notes from an ongoing upgrade of
my Verizon FIOS connection.

So far, I have been mostly happy with Verizon FIOS.  With the 
Actiontec wifi cable modem that they installed, not so much.  
It seems to cause problems with DNS and NAT.

Back when I had Comcan't, my traffic routed through an old
Thinkpad 600 configured as a linux router/firewall/openvpn-port.
The Actiontec now sits between that and the ONT (Optical Network
Terminal).  Since the ONT has a 100baseT port on it, I have my
own router, and don't have a TV,  the Actiontec is a buggy
waste of 13 watts.  Time to swap it out, step by step.

I spent way more time than planned over the last couple of days
changing the Actiontec from router mode to "bridge" mode, where
incoming packets fly straight through to the laptop router,
without NAT.  I figured out how to do it from this:

http://www.broadbandreports.com/forum/r17679150-Howto-make-ActionTec-MI424WR-a-network-bridge

Some steps are out of sequence, and it is a PITA making the router
release the DHCP lease (I finally just unplugged the MoCA cable
about 3 seconds after clicking release).  I left the cable
disconnected through the rest of the reconfiguration into
bridge mode, and reconnected everything when I was done.  It
took about 3 tries to get it right.

Now i'net packets are moving unmodified to the laptop/firewall.
I will be running a Personal Telco node on its own CAT5,
somewhere near the roof, and I want that on its own DMZ.  That
implies 3 ethernet ports to the firewall: external, DMZ, and
internal.  The old laptop has no onboard ethernet, and only two
PCMCIA slots for ethernet cards.  Fortunately, the laptop has a
USB2 port, and I found a brand new 3com USB-to-100baseT adapter
in the Free Geek store, which provides the third ethernet port.
Like most hardware these days, it was linux plug and play.

The DMZ is isolated from the internal network with a few lines
in iptables to discard packets going between those ethernet
ports.  If there is a better way, I would like to know about it.
I still need to modify named.conf so that only internal and vpn
machines know about each other.  When I am using wireless from
my laptop, I will connect through openvpn, just as if I am 
coming in from the outside world.


For now, the bridge mode Actiontec is still in the signal path
converting MoCA cable to ethernet.  The next step is to ask
Verizon support to switch the ONT from MoCA cable to 100baseT. 
Here is a plausible recipe:

http://www.dslreports.com/faq/15990

After verifying that ethernet works through the Actiontec's WAN
port (allowing me to go back to MoCA if this doesn't work), I
will remove the Actiontec and connect the firewall/router
directly to the ONT.  

BTW, I have a few spare PCMCIA ethernet cards;  if the ONT
somehow gets lightning-zapped, that will tend to zap the PCMCIA
ethernet card instead of the whole laptop.  PCMCIA cards are
also nice because if the bad guys are attacking, I can pop both
PCMCIA cards and physically isolate the firewall/laptop and my
internal network from the nasties, then reboot the laptop from
a live CD to look for damage.

If anyone else embarks on this process, I hope this is helpful.


Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

> On Tue, Aug 26, 2008 at 11:29 PM, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
> >
> > For now, the bridge mode Actiontec is still in the signal path
> > converting MoCA cable to ethernet.  The next step is to ask

On Wed, Aug 27, 2008 at 07:23:15AM -0700, Larry Brigman wrote:
> If you are now passing packets through you could take your laptop to
> the ONT and connect it directly to see what you would get.  I don't think
> there is much intelligence in the ONT.

I did try the Ethernet before doing all this, and it was powered
but not enabled.  They only activate one channel at a time, besides
the POTS, according to all the web pages I read, and I verified that.

Verizon FIOS technicians control the ONT directly, there is quite a
bit of smarts in the ONT, actually.  I called tech support, and the 
helpful fellow there got a network tech to change the connection 
from MoCA to Ethernet for me, and he camped on line while I finished
bringing things up.  He even released the DHCP lease a couple of
times, which is somewhat difficult with the Actiontec.

Three items: 
1) If you release the DHCP lease on the Actiontec, it will
take about 60 seconds to acquire a new one, and it will do so
automatically.  It is best to release the lease, wait 10 seconds,
then disconnect the Verizon side of the Actiontec.   Then hook
up your own router and request another DHCP lease with that.

2) The IP addresses assigned are all over the map;  at least 4
different address ranges so far.  Not a problem with dyndns pointing
at the dynamic IP, but interesting behavior anyway.  Their IP address
ranges are little chunks purchased from many different sources.

3) Before removing the Actiontec, I noted that the MoCA connection
base rate is 250Mbps.  They deliver video-on-demand with this.  
Right now, I have the minimum FIOS service, 5M up, 2M down.  If 
history repeats itself, that minimum will steadily increase.  Ten
years from now, when I decide that the 100Mbps ethernet is just
too slow ("a whole minute to download Ubuntu?  sheesh!"), I will
probably have to reactivate the MoCA cable connection.

Keith

general-purpose 1U servers. They run Opterons, not Xeons, but you can

Rogan Creswick wrote:
> I'm in the market for my first rackmount server, and I've realized
> that it's been far to long since I've built a machine from scratch. (I
> think I've been spoiled by laptops..).  This box won't be intensely
> used, by any means, it's just the cheapest form factor to set up in a
> friend's cabinet, so I'll have an always-on remote machine to host vcs
> repositories, my wiki, blog, etc.  Once in a while I'll need to use it
> for intense computation.
>
> What do you folks do when you need a new 1u box? (Does anyone have
> case/mobo/cpu/ram suggestions? Should I swallow my pride and get a
> Dell?)  I have a fairly good idea of what I want as far as hardware:
> dual quad-core ~2ghz processors, 4-8 gigs of ram, and roughly 1 TB of
> disk space, but other than that I want something that will work, but
> not break the bank (I'm not sure how to determine the power supply
> necessary, ensuring memory compatibility, proper cooling, etc.).  It
> will obviously be running Linux -- probably a bare, bare debian
> install with 2-3 vmware server instances that actually have
> interesting stuff installed :).
>
> Poking around at Newegg has made it clear that I probably need to do
> some reading if I decide to build my own, but first I'd like to see if
> it's even worth the effort, or if I should just go with a pre-built
> box.
>
>
>   
Go with a pre-built box if you can afford it and go with a known 
vendor.  The 1U form factor involves a lot of specialized engineering 
and you really need a vendor that has done a lot of volume to get it 
right.  Unless you go with a small disk form factor like the HP DL360, 
then you are going to have trouble getting 1TB of RAID 5 disk in the 
box, and please don't install it as RAID 0, you will really regret that 
decision when the array crashes and you need to restore 1TB from tape 
(your are planning on backing this up, right).  Hot swap is also good, 
but doesn't sound like a necessity in your case.

You will want dual power supplies, both for capacity and for 
redundancy.  Another reason not to do this yourself, the vendor has 
already done all the power consumption calculations for ALL the possible 
configurations.

Some specific models to consider.
http://h10010.www1.hp.com/wwpc/us/en/sm/WF25a/15351-15351-3328412-241644-241475-1121486.html
http://www.penguincomputing.com/products/linux/server/intel
http://www.appro.com/product/server_hyperserver_1200x.asp
http://www-03.ibm.com/systems/x/hardware/rack/x3550/index.html
http://www.sgi.com/products/servers/altix/xe/
http://www.verari.com/rackmount.asp

On Wed, Aug 27, 2008 at 1:32 PM, Michael Ewan <mhewan1 at comcast.net> wrote:
> then you are going to have trouble getting 1TB of RAID 5 disk in the
> box, and please don't install it as RAID 0, you will really regret that
> decision when the array crashes and you need to restore 1TB from tape
> (your are planning on backing this up, right).  Hot swap is also good,
> but doesn't sound like a necessity in your case.

I'm actually not planning on using raid, and for backups, I intend to
rsync nightly to an off-site server (the amount of disk I'm planning
to put in there is largely so I can use multiple VMs w/out being
worried about space).  The vast majority of the content will be
duplicated, since the VMs will be largely identical, and I'll have
off-site backups of them in a pristine state, along with incremental
backups of the configuration details, data, and code running on them.

I also could probably manage perfectly well with about 100 gigs, but
disk is cheap :)  My current server is a shared penguin computing 1u,
and we were recently bitten by the raid/lvm configuration -- some
content was corrupted and mirrored across the raid before we noticed.
When we *did* notice, the system went down and would no longer boot.
We would have lost the (software) raid conf, if it weren't for an
off-site backup (or good memory... I wasn't actually involved in that
restoration).  In any case, I am not in a situation where that degree
of high-availability is worth the premium it costs--periodic backups
are sufficient to manage the times when something goes down.

Re: penguin computing -- I like the company, and the issues we've had
with our current penguin 1u are probably due to environmental problems
(poor cooling at the colo).  My only hesitation is that it looks like
the same hardware can be had from Dell for significantly less (there
is about a $400 difference between the Altus 650 and a similarly
configured PowerEdge SC1435 right now).

Since neither company offers an OS I am comfortable with, I'll be
reinstalling on my own anyway. (And probably buying the disk(s) from
newegg, to save another couple hundred.)  I would sleep better buying
from penguin computing though :)

> You will want dual power supplies, both for capacity and for
> redundancy.  Another reason not to do this yourself, the vendor has
> already done all the power consumption calculations for ALL the possible
> configurations.

Very good point...

--Rogan

To add to the Options:  If you uncheck 'drawings' and click Notes, you'll
get one page per slide with 1/2 the space devoted to notes and 1/2 devoted
to the slide. If 'drawings' is left checked, you'll get a page for the slide
and a second page will be as described above.

Also,right  clicking the slides in the left column lets you change the
background so you can prevent printing that.

Robert

From bobby_miesen at yahoo.com  Wed Aug 27 18:19:40 2008
From: bobby_miesen at yahoo.com (Robert Miesen)
Date: Wed, 27 Aug 2008 18:19:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] C programming
In-Reply-To: <1219881555.6261.2.camel@rich-desktop>
References: <1219881555.6261.2.camel@rich-desktop>
Message-ID: <48B5FD2C.1050901@yahoo.com>

Another problem I've noticed with your program: you failed to declare 
the return type of main. It should look like this:

    int
    main( int argc, char[] *argv) {
    ...

    If you don't know already, the int means that the main method 
returns an integer (the return value of the program), the argc denotes 
the number of arguments passed to the program (including the name of the 
program itself, which means that argc is always >= 1), and the argv is a 
collection stringized command-line arguments passed to the program, 
which includes the name of the program itself.

    Try making your sample program look like the beginning of the main 
function above and it should compile just fine. If it doesn't, try 
subbing out the 'char[] *argv' with 'char **argv' and that should do the 
trick.

    Hope this helps.

From daehlie at gmail.com  Wed Aug 27 18:49:19 2008
From: daehlie at gmail.com (Kirk Harr)
Date: Wed, 27 Aug 2008 18:49:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] 1u server advice..
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80808271411s667ef518ka13ff2fae5f4f13e@mail.gmail.com>
References: <c834ec80808271224o29604a39gf4818eec6b03f9b2@mail.gmail.com>
	<48B5B9D0.3000306@comcast.net>
	<c834ec80808271411s667ef518ka13ff2fae5f4f13e@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <6edf64990808271849l78fe6d45hd1741fb2d808d6e0@mail.gmail.com>

If you are going to run multiple VMs off a single disk system you want to
watch out for I/O contention issues.  I would suggest a RAID 10 setup with
either 4 or 8 disks that will give you redundancy and max concurrent I/O.
RAID 5 is fine, but 10 on a 4 disk set would be ideal. I also suggest
hardware RAID in lieu of software to squeeze every clock cycle out of the
CPUs for the VMs.

Kirk Harr

> I'm actually not planning on using raid, and for backups, I intend to

> duplicated, since the VMs will be largely identical, and I'll have
> off-site backups of them in a pristine state, along with incremental
> backups of the configuration details, data, and code running on them.
>
> I also could probably manage perfectly well with about 100 gigs, but
> disk is cheap :)  My current server is a shared penguin computing 1u,
> and we were recently bitten by the raid/lvm configuration -- some
> content was corrupted and mirrored across the raid before we noticed.
> When we *did* notice, the system went down and would no longer boot.
> We would have lost the (software) raid conf, if it weren't for an
> off-site backup (or good memory... I wasn't actually involved in that
> restoration).  In any case, I am not in a situation where that degree
> of high-availability is worth the premium it costs--periodic backups
> are sufficient to manage the times when something goes down.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Aug 27 19:49:03 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 27 Aug 2008 19:49:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Verizon FIOS, swapping out the Actiontec cable modem (2)
In-Reply-To: <200808271717.38239.aaron@madebyai.com>
References: <20080827062930.GA15653@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<48B5AE10.1080708@seitzassoc.com>

Message-ID: <20080828024903.GA21405@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Aug 27, 2008 at 05:17:32PM -0700, Aaron Ten Clay wrote:
> If you know anyone else getting FiOS, you might want to recommend they have 
> ethernet run into their home instead of coax. That's what I did. They won't 
> charge extra at install time and it's much easier to remove the actiontec 
> router - just unplug the cat5e and connect to a new linux PC for routing :)

I had the cat5 there, waiting to plug into the ONT, but the installer
insisted on running coax to an Actiontec.  Friendly enough fellow,
but it had to be done his way.  The coax is needed to feed video,
and MoCA is needed for video-on-demand, and the installer couldn't
imagine anyone surviving without a boob tube. 

I didn't fight too hard;  I didn't want him doing a bad job on
something he did not understand.  I would have preferred being
able to ask for the ethernet option when I scheduled the install; 
then they could have sent a contractor that was familiar with cat5
installs.  It was only later, when I realized what a PITA the
Actiontec was, that I realized I should have pushed harder for
ethernet instead of MoCA - during the installation request.

When people sign up for internet-only FIOS, they should make very
clear that they want the ethernet option installed, without the
Actiontec.  The few that succeed should tell us exactly what they
said, so we can share the magic incantation with others.  When an
installer shows up and actually installs cat5 connections, GET 
THEIR NAMES so the next folks that sign up can ask for the
installers that know how.

Keith

Here is another vendor to consider, and an example of a 4 disk RAID system
in 1U:
http://eracks.com/products/Xeon%20Systems/config?sku=NOCONA

This is just one example system, this one has 2 x dual core Xeons, they can
do pretty much whatever you want configuration-wise.
3ware hardware RAID controller, up to 16GB RAM, etc etc.
For more than 4 disks, or 4 disks with dual redundant power supplies, would
need to be 2U or more.
If you aren't dead set on 1U, and are willing to go 2U, that opens up a lot
more configuration possibilities.

eRacks is a Linux/BSD system vendor (rackmounts, desktops, laptops), located
in So. CA but with business all over.

From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Thu Aug 28 03:55:57 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Thu, 28 Aug 2008 06:55:57 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] C programming
In-Reply-To: <1219881555.6261.2.camel@rich-desktop>
References: <1219881555.6261.2.camel@rich-desktop>
Message-ID: <1219920957.9038.20.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Wed, 2008-08-27 at 16:59 -0700, rich wrote:
> main(){
>     printf("hello, world.\n");
> }

Several people have already explained that you need
#include <stdio.h>

It might be good at this point to explain why the book your reading may
not have mentioned that. In the past, GCC was less picky than it is now.
Once upon a time, the above code would have compiled. As GCC has
improved, its ability to recognize errors and provide warning has
improved. While this results in higher quality code in the long term, in
the short term it makes GCC more annoying. If you find your book
repeatedly omits information necessary to compile with a modern GCC,
perhaps you will need to find a more correct book.

May I also suggest some flags to make GCC even pickier? This will help
you to learn good habits from the beginning.

gcc -pedantic -Werror -Wall -Wextra -Wfloat-equal -Wformat=2
-Wno-unused-parameter

This is only a taste of what GCC can do. Once you're more comfortable
with C, you may want to add extra checks like:
-Wtraditional -Wwrite-strings

I don't know how much you care about backwards compatibility. I don't
care much so I prefer coding against C99 instead of GNU C90, even though
GCC doesn't fully implement C99 yet.
-std=c99

You'll find that C is a grotty language with a complex history, but it's
also beautiful in its own way. Have a lot of fun!

Rogan Creswick wrote:
> I'm in the market for my first rackmount server, and I've realized
> that it's been far to long since I've built a machine from scratch.

If you're going to do a 1U then I suggest buying a built unit. You can 
save a lot of money by buying a barebone, then it still gives you a lot 
of control over the server in terms of what you want. 1U's are too 
specialized. The DIY stuff I've seen just doesn't compare to even decent 
quality desktop parts. Go to Newegg and look at their barebone systems 
from SuperMicro, Intel, and possibly ASUS, though I've never used ASUS 
for a server. Oh, and get ready for "loud".

-- 
- m0gely

From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)

m0gely (m0gely at telestream.com) typed this ...
> Go to Newegg and look at their barebone systems 
> from SuperMicro, Intel, and possibly ASUS, though I've never used ASUS 
> for a server. Oh, and get ready for "loud".

I recently built up one of these:

  http://www.newegg.com/Product/Product.aspx?Item=N82E16816110030

It runs nearly silent, but still plenty cool. Only a single socket,
though. I was very impressed with the quality. I have a client who specs
nothing but Supermicro servers, and they've never had a hardware
failure. So I was planning to go the same way until I found this ASUS. 

It's also got a slot for an IPMI card, but I couldn't find anyone
selling the card at the time, 2 months ago.

With 8G RAM, 2 SATA2 drives and a quad-core Xeon at 2.4GHz, my
Kill-a-watt showed .6A draw at idle. Measure your systems and see how
that compares.

 > > It might be good at this point to explain why the book your reading may
 > > not have mentioned that. In the past, GCC was less picky than it is now.
 > > Once upon a time, the above code would have compiled. As GCC has
 > > improved, its ability to recognize errors and provide warning has
 > > improved. While this results in higher quality code in the long term, in
 > > the short term it makes GCC more annoying. If you find your book
 > > repeatedly omits information necessary to compile with a modern GCC,
 > > perhaps you will need to find a more correct book.
 > > 
 > > May I also suggest some flags to make GCC even pickier? This will help
 > > you to learn good habits from the beginning.
 > > 
 > > gcc -pedantic -Werror -Wall -Wextra -Wfloat-equal -Wformat=2
 > > -Wno-unused-parameter
 > > 
 > > This is only a taste of what GCC can do. Once you're more comfortable
 > > with C, you may want to add extra checks like:
 > > -Wtraditional -Wwrite-strings
 > > 
 > > I don't know how much you care about backwards compatibility. I don't
 > > care much so I prefer coding against C99 instead of GNU C90, even though
 > > GCC doesn't fully implement C99 yet.
 > > -std=c99
 > > 
 > > You'll find that C is a grotty language with a complex history, but it's
 > > also beautiful in its own way. Have a lot of fun!
 > > 

Typical of a server build.  If you can't build a C program on the box,
neither can an attacker.
-- 
Carlos

>>>> then dispatches to the correct service
>>>> routine.
>>>>  
>>>> If when the executive is called, and no form data is found, the 
>>>> status message you saw is returned - so it
>>>> appears that two things are going wrong:
>>>>  
>>>> (1) As soon as the HTML page is loaded, (or maybe even prior to be 
>>>> completely loaded), the form is
>>>>      being submitted.
>>>>  
>>>> (2) When the form is submitted, none of the data in the form is 
>>>> being transmitted.
>>>>  
>>>>  
>>>> I just checked this on a PC with both IE7.0 and Firefox2.0 and it 
>>>> works OK.  A few things to check though:
>>>>  
>>>> (a) make sure Javascript is enabled
>>>> (b) make sure you are using a current version of Firefox
>>>>  
>>>> If this is all true, I would file a problem report with Firefox 
>>>> directly, giving them the URL of my page for them
>>>> to test on.  It sounds like the browser is misbehaving.
>>>>  
>>>> I MIGHT have access to Firefox for Linux on the systems in my main 
>>>> office.  We have lots of linux systems
>>>> there and one might have Firefox installed.  When I am back there 
>>>> tommorrow, I'll give it a try.
>>>>  
>>>> -Bob
>>>>  
>>>> it out on our Linux workstatt
>>>>

On Aug 28, 2008, at 1:18 PM, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> On Thu, 28 Aug 2008, Rich Shepard wrote:
>> On Thu, 28 Aug 2008, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
>>> Typical of a server build.  If you can't build a C program on the  
>>> box,
>>> neither can an attacker.
>>
>>   Wouldn't this also by typical of an installation that defaults  
>> to end
>> users? They most likely would not build any new application from  
>> source;
>> rather, they'd download the binary from a ubunty repository.
>>
>
> Yes, except with a desktop system you're protecting the user from
> himself, not others.  Just kidding.  But seriously, I have seen people
> struggle with VMWare, for example, because there was no gcc and the  
> user
> didn't know what gcc was or why it was missing.  "But it installs on
> redhat!", they say.  "Isn't fedora just like redhat?"  and so on.

Ah, dependency hell. Over the years I've gradually built up recipes  
to copy/paste in the command line, just to have the right packages,  
and not waste time re-finding stuff. Here's my current base (without  
the freetds, that's another 5 lines, from a non-official repository)  
single *line* for getting the dependencies for rebuilding my stock  
PHP application server rpm's (off of the rpmforge spec):

# yum install wget nano make autoconf automake rpm-build postfix  
fileutils file libtool gcc cpp gcc-c++ perl-DBI libc-client-devel  
libstdc++-devel bzip2-devel curl-devel db4-devel expat-devel gmp- 
devel aspell-devel httpd-devel libjpeg-devel libpng-devel pam-devel  
openssl-devel sqlite-devel zlib-devel pcre-devel krb5-devel cyrus- 
sasl-devel openldap-devel postgresql-devel unixODBC-devel libxml2- 
devel net-snmp-devel libxslt-devel libxml2-devel expat-devel ncurses- 
devel gd-devel freetype-devel readline-devel libmcrypt-devel libtidy- 
devel freetds-devel mhash-devel

...and sometimes I still have flashbacks to the "early days" of  
package management, when a single build macro script could easily run  
thousands of lines long... *shudder*.

On a related note, I was once asked (in 2006!) in a tech job  
interview (position: Security Researcher): "What would you do if you  
couldn't install a piece of software because of a missing library,  
that you couldn't get with a package manager?"

I asked if it was a trick question, when they assured me it wasn't, I  
hesitantly said "Uhm, er, download the sources, edit the config and  
compile scripts as needed to match the target platform, and then make  
and make install it?" Out of all the people they had interviewed, I  
was apparently the *only* one who even mentioned actually downloading  
and building it myself. I got the job. :)

Compiling by hand is rapidly becoming a lost art.... again....

-Bop

On Thu, 28 Aug 2008, Ronald Chmara wrote:

> building it myself. I got the job. :)
>
> Compiling by hand is rapidly becoming a lost art.... again....

Bop,

   I'm sorry to read that. It is so simple and takes so little time on modern
systems that everyone should do it just for the knowledge.

   My experience (based on six years of Red Hat followed by five years with
Slackware) has been that the latter is so easy for local builds that it's my
accepted practice. There is a very active group who participate in (and
manage) slackbuilds.org. Their efforts provide scripts to build almost
everything into a Slackware package using the same standards and criteria as
the core distribution does.

   Using a SlackBuild script I not only build locally (and with the proper
dependencies for each distribution version), but intallation and upgrade (or
removal when desired) is handled by the package management system. Best of
both worlds.

   Wonder why folks fear and avoid the 'configure; make; make install' dance.

Rich

On Fri, 2008-08-29 at 06:20 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    Wonder why folks fear and avoid the 'configure; make; make install' dance.

Well, for openers, because not every package conforms to that schema,
and *effort* is required from the *developers* of the package to make it
that easy for the user. Second, because when it croaks, the user now has
to interact with the developers.

As you probably recall, I run Gentoo, which is mostly a
build-from-source distro, whose "packages" are mostly shell scripts to
build from source. I'm so used to it now, I don't give it a second
thought, but when I migrated from Debian (Woody) to Gentoo (1.4), my
compute and download resources were much less powerful than they are
today, and compile times were a significant expense.
> 
> Rich
> 
-- 
M. Edward (Ed) Borasky
ruby-perspectives.blogspot.com

It needed to be said, thank you for saying it. Unless you're a developer
or a hobbyist looking to have fun, installing from source is probably
not a wise decision. While it may not be hard to do, the long term

the most critical parts of one's infrastructure.

On Fri, 2008-08-29 at 09:04 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Fri, 29 Aug 2008, Stuart Jansen wrote:
> > It needed to be said, thank you for saying it. Unless you're a developer
> > or a hobbyist looking to have fun, installing from source is probably not
> > a wise decision. While it may not be hard to do, the long term maintenance
> > cost is much higher and can probably only be justified for the most
> > critical parts of one's infrastructure.
> 
>    Consider using checkinstall. It replaces 'make install' and offers the
> options of creating a package in .rpm, .deb, or .tga format (Red Hat/Fedora,
> Debian, and Slackware formats). Then upgrades or removals use the package
> management tools.

I'm familiar with checkinstall, however it has several deficiencies:

1) It only records metadata that can be programmaticly captured. For
example, it can determine required and provided symbols but it can't
determine required or provided capabilities such as "webserver". Nor, as
far as I'm aware, can it distinguish between reguirements vs.
suggestions as supported by .deb packages.

2) It does not strictly follow distribution specific conventions. Most
distributions include patches to integrate software with the system. As
a result, such packages are at best second class citizens.

3) Most importantly, you are still accepting responsibility for watching
for security and other bug fixes. Performing Q&A. Possibly backporting
patches when an upgrade is too disruptive. Etc. Doing so only makes
sense for software that is truly unique and critical to your
infrastructure. When such is the case, one might as well take a little
extra time and care to package software with the tools and conventions
of one's distro.
