On Fri, 29 Aug 2008, Stuart Jansen wrote:

> I'm familiar with checkinstall, however it has several deficiencies:

   Quite true.

   While I've no familiarity at all with Debian and its offspring, I used to
know how to write a .spec file to create a Red Hat .rpm and I use
(occasionally writing one myself) a SlackBuild script.

   If the application is important, then it should be worth the effort to get
a good package building script for it. Bug fixes and security uprgrades can
be built using the same script and installed using the distribution's
package management tools.

   My original point is that there are tools available build package
management compatible installation/upgrade files, so it's unfortunate that
folks don't take the time to learn and benefit from their use.

Rich

Aug 29 10:30:04 salmo postfix/smtpd[5938]: warning: wap.appl-ecosys.com[192.168.55.200]:
  SASL PLAIN authentication failed: generic failure

   So, it's with my postfix setup. Reading again the SASL_README file,
/usr/lib/sasl2/smtpd.conf contains:

# Global parameters
log_level: 4
pwcheck_method: saslauthd
mech_list: PLAIN LOGIN

On Fri, Aug 29, 2008 at 12:52 PM, Russell Senior
<russell at personaltelco.net> wrote:
>>>>>> "Dan" == Dan Young <danielmyoung at gmail.com> writes:
>
>>> Wonder why folks fear and avoid the 'configure; make; make install'
>>> dance.
>
> Dan> I don't fear 'make install', but I sure appreciate the lowered
> Dan> barrier to entry to trying new software provided by package
> Dan> management that lets me trivially remove it.
>
> I sometimes do 'configure --prefix=/packages/foo ; make ; make
> install', then I can trivially remove it with: 'rm -rf /packages/foo'

there are a ton of systems that facilitate this kind of behavior, some
of which i've used and loved:

gnu graft - http://freshmeat.net/projects/graft/ (makes symlink farms
from the private dir, uninstalls cleanly)
opt_depot - http://www.arlut.utexas.edu/csd/opt_depot/
(enterprise-grade multiplatform capable)

and then systems to take advantage of the fact that you can now have a
zillion versions of anything installed (yeah, i liked the old firefox
better, but would rather modify my environment to run it than making
an alias or naming the script firefox2): ...which was called
"modules", which is now ungoogle-able :/

i love setting things up this way myself.  i even re-abstracted some
packages so i could easily update the system version of glib and
others without yanking them out from under gnome (which i gave its own
private /packages/gnome/* hierarchy where i built a version of glib i
left alone).

truly, this system used to work better before the shenanigans with
library versioning and non-backward or forward compatibility crept
into the post-gnome world (freetype was a frequent offender, but glib
did it a few times too and so did some KDE-required libs).

due to all of those experiences, i have not tried to build gnome or
kde from source in the past 2 years, and do love my package manager
more...

suggests that this is outdated. From the "outdated" clamscan, I have no
viruses.

return address - what do you do?
2) What approach should I take to virus / worm checking?

Mike

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Fri Aug 29 13:55:54 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Fri, 29 Aug 2008 13:55:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Virus / Worm Check
In-Reply-To: <1220041298.8431.8.camel@UbuntuHH>
References: <1220041298.8431.8.camel@UbuntuHH>
Message-ID: <20080829205554.GE2544@sentinelchicken.org>

> 1) If you've been victimized by a spammer who has used your email as a
> return address - what do you do?

Nothing.  There's nothing stopping anyone from spoofing your email
address.  The best thing you can do is try to prevent the spammers from
getting your email address in the first place.

Those mail servers that are bouncing messages back to you because
they're considered spam are configured by fools.  They're just doubling
the amount of traffic created by the spam.  Return addresses are
meaningless when it comes to spam.


> 2) What approach should I take to virus / worm checking?

It's highly unlikely that you have a virus or worm.  There have only
been a handful of Linux/Unix based worms (compared to the numbers of
Windows ones at least) and none recently that I'm aware of.  If you were
compromised, it's unlikely an attacker would just send spam as you from
a Linux box.  They'd probably be using it for much more interesting
things, such as a command and control box or an anonymizing proxy, etc.
No point in trying to tip you off by sending emails in your name.  It's
also highly unlikely that any AV software would catch what you're
compromised with (if you were).  You could try running rootkit hunter
and chkrootkit to see if anything comes up fishy.


HTH,
tim

> On Fri, 29 Aug 2008, Stuart Jansen wrote:

>> It needed to be said, thank you for saying it. Unless you're a developer
>> or a hobbyist looking to have fun, installing from source is probably not
>> a wise decision. While it may not be hard to do, the long term maintenance
>> cost is much higher and can probably only be justified for the most
>> critical parts of one's infrastructure.
>
> Stuart,
>
>   Consider using checkinstall. It replaces 'make install' and offers the
> options of creating a package in .rpm, .deb, or .tga format (Red Hat/Fedora,
> Debian, and Slackware formats). Then upgrades or removals use the package
> management tools.
>
> Rich

Note: checkinstall only works for 32bit systems.  Even the source
doesn't build correctly
for 64 bit systems.

It's true that mail functionality is not broken, but my point is that you're
not likely to break said mail functionality by playing with saslauthd.
Theoretically (aka irrelevantly), it's the function of sending mail from
another host, authenticating via saslauthd, that is broken. I can only
assume from the context that you only have the one user sending mail from
another host.

If that is the case, you may want to consider finding another way to
authenticate that host.

If you would like us to help with the remaining issue, it would be helpful
to know what changes were made, and what effect (if any) those changes had
on the previously reported symptoms.

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Fri Aug 29 19:42:24 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Fri, 29 Aug 2008 19:42:24 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Virus / Worm Check
In-Reply-To: <1220063327.8431.26.camel@UbuntuHH>
References: <1220041298.8431.8.camel@UbuntuHH>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0808291423360.16246@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<1220063327.8431.26.camel@UbuntuHH>
Message-ID: <20080830024224.GF2544@sentinelchicken.org>

> I appreciate everyone's help. In any case, I've figured out enough
> keywords and phrases to filter out 95%+ of the bounce-back (hopefully
> keeping the important stuff). If this happens again, I might just have
> to bite the bullet and start configuring my own email server for real. 

You don't want to get into the business of filtering keywords manually.
That's just a rabbit's hole.  For content filtering, you're going to
want to use a bayesian filter of some variety.  There's quite a few out
there, some built into mail clients, some stand-alone.  I personally use
CRM114, which is incrediby accurate, but takes some feeding and
maintenance.  You could plausibly use it without a mail server.  If you
do set up a mail server, you'll want to have something set up to block
known-evil source IPs.

> Any recommendations? I've played with sendmail and Postfix, hear good
> things about qmail.

I use qmail.  It's fast, very secure and quite simple.  However, it's
not really actively maintained, so you have to wade through a plethora
of third party patches when you want this or that feature.  (That might
start to improve soon, since it's been released into the public domain,
but only time will tell.)  I don't recommend sendmail, simply due to
it's blatantly poor security track record.  Postfix is pretty popular
and easy to set up, so it might be a good first mail server to try out.

good luck,
tim

MJang wrote:

>>
> 
> Any recommendations? I've played with sendmail and Postfix, hear good
> things about qmail.

I have run my own mail server since the mid 80's (sometimes wondering 
why).  I used to use sendmail when I was running HP-UX but moved 
gleefully to postfix/spamassassin on Linux (RH9, RHEL, FC4 then settled 
on Debian).  It takes a little set-up and "training" the baysian system 
and occasional maintenance (usually minor tweaking depending on the kind 
of spam you receive and how smart the spammers are) but is well 
supported and, best of all, it works.

what I would do for all DNS resolution.

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Sun Aug 31 18:34:13 2008
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (robinson-west user)
Date: Sun, 31 Aug 2008 18:34:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS question
In-Reply-To: <d09308a80808311630va6499c6j19a220099e1a4751@mail.gmail.com>
References: <d09308a80808311630va6499c6j19a220099e1a4751@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1220232853.24344.1.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

On Sun, 2008-08-31 at 16:30 -0700, Sean Whitney wrote:
> I have a webserver / DNS server in my DMZ, which servers up different DNS
> results depending on the source.  So external DNS queries to my domain get
> my public IP, while DNS queries from my internal network to the server
> returns the internal DMZ IP address.
> 
> What do I need to do to have my local DNS servers (those in my internal
> network) look first at the DMZ server for DNS resolution (for these domains)
> and if it doesn't return something (say for google) then look on the
> Internet.
> 
> I don't think this is a forwarder entry in the named.conf file, as this is
> what I would do for all DNS resolution.
> 
> Or is there another method I should be using.
> 
> 
> Sean

     // Root server hints.
     zone "."{
           type hint;
           file "db.cache";
     };

Should go in your local view if you are using Bind 9.
The db.cache file is a list of the root nameservers on
the Internet and it needs to be kept up to date.  Your
local and external view should share the same db.cache.

     -- Michael C. Robinson

On Aug 31, 2008, at 4:30 PM, Sean Whitney wrote:

> I have a webserver / DNS server in my DMZ, which servers up  
> different DNS
> results depending on the source.  So external DNS queries to my  
> domain get
> my public IP, while DNS queries from my internal network to the server
> returns the internal DMZ IP address.
>
> What do I need to do to have my local DNS servers (those in my  
> internal
> network) look first at the DMZ server for DNS resolution (for these  
> domains)
> and if it doesn't return something (say for google) then look on the
> Internet.
>
> I don't think this is a forwarder entry in the named.conf file, as  
> this is
> what I would do for all DNS resolution.
>
> Or is there another method I should be using.

Sounds like a split-horizon situation to me, with the horizon master  
set as the default querying server... only you're adding in a layer  
of *multiple* split horizons, where the DMZ server knows about this  
internal/external situation, but other internal severs are blind to  
this, or only *some* of the servers know the split-horizon results?

Is there some technical reason for running a flock of different DNS  
servers, instead of one or two powerful ones in the DMZ that handle  
all internal *and* external requests?

Can your internal DNS machines act as slaves (for internal zones) to  
your DMZ server, and have the internals do split-horizon?

Is perhaps "Split Horizon DNS" the magic words you needed to know, in  
order to google this easily? :)

-Bop
